         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Area_code_951" title="Area code 951">
          951
         </a>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Greater Los Angeles
       </b>
       is the second-largest urban region in the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
        United States
       </a>
       , encompassing five counties in
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_California" title="Southern California">
        southern California
       </a>
       , extending from
       <a href="/wiki/Ventura_County,_California" title="Ventura County, California">
        Ventura County
       </a>
       in the west to
       <a href="/wiki/San_Bernardino_County,_California" title="San Bernardino County, California">
        San Bernardino County
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Riverside_County,_California" title="Riverside County, California">
        Riverside County
       </a>
       on the east, with
       <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_County,_California" title="Los Angeles County, California">
        Los Angeles County
       </a>
       in the center and
       <a href="/wiki/Orange_County,_California" title="Orange County, California">
        Orange County
       </a>
       to the southeast.
      </p>
      <p>
       Throughout the 20th century, it was one of the fastest-growing regions in the United States, although growth has slowed since 2000. As of the
       <a href="/wiki/2010_United_States_Census" title="2010 United States Census">
        2010 U.S. Census
       </a>
       , the Los Angeles
       <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_statistical_area" title="Metropolitan statistical area">
        Metropolitan Statistical Area
       </a>
       had a population of nearly 13 million residents. Meanwhile, the larger metropolitan region's population at the 2010 census was estimated to be over 17.8 million residents, and a 2015 estimate reported a population of about 18.7 million.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2015combinedest_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-2015combinedest-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Either definition makes it the second largest metropolitan region in the country, behind the
       <a href="/wiki/New_York_metropolitan_area" title="New York metropolitan area">
        New York metropolitan area
       </a>
       , as well as one of the largest
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urban_agglomeration" title="Urban agglomeration">
        urban agglomerations
       </a>
       in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The agglomeration of the
       <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">
        urbanized
       </a>
       Greater Los Angeles area surrounds the urban core of Los Angeles County. The regional term is defined to refer to the more-or-less continuously urbanized area stretching from Ventura County to the southern border of Orange County and from the Pacific Ocean to the Coachella Valley in the Inland Empire.
      </p>
      <p>
       The US Census Bureau defines the
       <b>
        Los Angeles-Long Beach, CA
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combined_Statistical_Area" title="Combined Statistical Area">
         Combined Statistical Area
        </a>
       </b>
       as including the entire
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_County" title="Los Angeles County">
        Los Angeles County
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ventura_County,_California" title="Ventura County, California">
        Ventura County
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Orange_County,_California" title="Orange County, California">
        Orange County
       </a>
       and the two counties of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inland_Empire_(California)" title="Inland Empire (California)">
        Inland Empire
       </a>
       . However, this Census definition includes large, sparsely populated and primarily desert swaths of Los Angeles, San Bernardino and Riverside counties that are not part of the urbanized region. The term "Greater Los Angeles" does not include
       <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_County,_California" title="San Diego County, California">
        San Diego County
       </a>
       , whose urbanized area is separated from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Clemente" title="San Clemente">
        San Clemente
       </a>
       , the southernmost contiguous urbanized area south of Los Angeles, by a 16.4-mile (26.4 km) stretch of the
       <a href="/wiki/Marine_Corps_Base_Camp_Pendleton" title="Marine Corps Base Camp Pendleton">
        Marine Corps Base Camp Pendleton
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       According to the U.S. Census Bureau, the Los Angeles metropolitan area has a total area of 4,850 square miles (12,561 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       ), while the wider combined statistical area covers 33,954 square miles (87,940 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       ), making it the largest metropolitan region in the United States by land area. However, more than half of this area lies in the sparsely populated eastern areas of Riverside and San Bernardino counties. In addition to being the nexus of the world's largest entertainment industry, Greater Los Angeles is also a global center of business, international trade, education, media, fashion, tourism, science and technology, sports, and transportation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:left; float:left; width:auto; background:none; margin-bottom:0.5em; padding:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0;">

      <div style="clear:both;">

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">
        Geography
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geography">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb noresize tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:90%; max-width:1208px;">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="">
        <div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Los_Angeles_Basin_from_Mulholland_Pan.jpg" title="The Los Angeles Basin, viewed south from Mulholland Drive. From left to right can be seen the Santa Ana Mountains / Saddleback (horizon), downtown L.A., the Hollywood Bowl (foreground), Mid-Wilshire, Long Beach – Palos Verdes (background), Catalina Island (horizon), the Southbay and Pacific Ocean.">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="3845" height="479" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Los_Angeles_Basin_from_Mulholland_Pan.jpg/1200px-Los_Angeles_Basin_from_Mulholland_Pan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Los_Angeles_Basin_from_Mulholland_Pan.jpg/1800px-Los_Angeles_Basin_from_Mulholland_Pan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Los_Angeles_Basin_from_Mulholland_Pan.jpg/2400px-Los_Angeles_Basin_from_Mulholland_Pan.jpg 2x" width="1200"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Los_Angeles_Basin_from_Mulholland_Pan.jpg" title="File:Los Angeles Basin from Mulholland Pan.jpg">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Basin" title="Los Angeles Basin">
          Los Angeles Basin
         </a>
         , viewed south from
         <a href="/wiki/Mulholland_Drive" title="Mulholland Drive">
          Mulholland Drive
         </a>
         . From left to right can be seen the
         <a href="/wiki/Santa_Ana_Mountains" title="Santa Ana Mountains">
          Santa Ana Mountains
         </a>
         /
         <a href="/wiki/Saddleback_(Orange_County,_California)" title="Saddleback (Orange County, California)">
          Saddleback
         </a>
         (horizon),
         <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Los_Angeles" title="Downtown Los Angeles">
          downtown L.A.
         </a>
         , the
         <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Bowl" title="Hollywood Bowl">
          Hollywood Bowl
         </a>
         (foreground),
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-Wilshire" title="Mid-Wilshire">
          Mid-Wilshire
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Long_Beach,_California" title="Long Beach, California">
          Long Beach
         </a>
         –
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palos_Verdes" title="Palos Verdes">
          Palos Verdes
         </a>
         (background),
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santa_Catalina_Island,_California" title="Santa Catalina Island, California">
          Catalina Island
         </a>
         (horizon), the
         <a href="/wiki/South_Bay,_Los_Angeles" title="South Bay, Los Angeles">
          Southbay
         </a>
         and Pacific Ocean.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_form">
        Urban form
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Urban form">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Att_building_LA.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Att_building_LA.jpg/220px-Att_building_LA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Att_building_LA.jpg/330px-Att_building_LA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Att_building_LA.jpg/440px-Att_building_LA.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Att_building_LA.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Skyscrapers in
         <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Los_Angeles" title="Downtown Los Angeles">
          Downtown Los Angeles
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Los Angeles has a long-standing reputation for
       <a href="/wiki/Urban_sprawl" title="Urban sprawl">
        sprawl
       </a>
       . The area is in fact sprawling, but according to the 2000 census, the "Los Angeles-Long Beach-Anaheim"
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_urban_area" title="United States urban area">
        Urbanized Area
       </a>
       (but not counting the portions of the Inland Empire in the Greater Los Angeles Area) had a
       <a href="/wiki/Population_density" title="Population density">
        population density
       </a>
       of 7,068 inhabitants per square mile (2,729/km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       ), covering 1,668 square miles (4,320 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       ) of land area, making it the most densely populated Urbanized Area (as defined by the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">
        United States Census Bureau
       </a>
       ) in the United States.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For comparison, the
       <a href="/wiki/New_York_metropolitan_area" title="New York metropolitan area">
        "New York–Newark" Urbanized Area
       </a>
       as a whole had a population density of 5,309 per square mile (2,050/km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       ), covering 3,353 square miles (8,684 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       ) of land area.
      </p>
      <p>
       Los Angeles' sprawl may originate in the region's
       <a href="/wiki/Decentralization" title="Decentralization">
        decentralized
       </a>
       structure. Its major commercial, financial, and cultural institutions are geographically dispersed rather than being concentrated in a single
       <a href="/wiki/Downtown" title="Downtown">
        downtown
       </a>
       or central area. Also, the population
       <a href="/wiki/Population_density" title="Population density">
        density
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">
        Los Angeles
       </a>
       proper is low (approximately 8,100 people per square mile) when compared to some other large American cities such as
       <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">
        New York
       </a>
       (27,500),
       <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">
        San Francisco
       </a>
       (17,000),
       <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">
        Boston
       </a>
       (13,300), and
       <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">
        Chicago
       </a>
       (11,800).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Densities are particularly high within a 5-mile radius of downtown, where some neighborhoods exceed 20,000 people per square mile.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="No source provided for previous edit. Appears to be original research (May 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       What gives the entire Los Angeles metro region a high density is the fact that many of the city's suburbs and satellites cities have high density rates.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Within its urbanized areas, Los Angeles is noted for having small lot sizes and low-rise buildings. Buildings in the area are low when compared to other large cities, mainly due to zoning regulations. Los Angeles became a major city just as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Electric_Railway" title="Pacific Electric Railway">
        Pacific Electric Railway
       </a>
       spread population to smaller cities much as interurbans did in East Coast cities. In the first decades of the twentieth century, the area was marked by a network of fairly dense but separate cities linked by rail. The ascendance of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">
        automobile
       </a>
       helped fill in the gaps between these commuter towns with lower-density settlements.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_city_dev.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="161" data-file-width="250" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/La_city_dev.jpg" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         A flat land area in the Greater Los Angeles Area completely filled with houses, buildings, roads, and freeways
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Starting in the early twentieth century, there was a large growth in population on the western edges of the city moving to the
       <a href="/wiki/San_Fernando_Valley" title="San Fernando Valley">
        San Fernando Valley
       </a>
       and out into the
       <a href="/wiki/Conejo_Valley" title="Conejo Valley">
        Conejo Valley
       </a>
       in eastern
       <a href="/wiki/Ventura_County,_California" title="Ventura County, California">
        Ventura County
       </a>
       . Many working class whites
       <a href="/wiki/White_flight" title="White flight">
        migrated
       </a>
       to this area during the 1960s and 1970s out of East and Central Los Angeles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As a result, there was a large growth in population into the Conejo Valley and into Ventura County through the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Route_101_(California)" title="U.S. Route 101 (California)">
        US 101
       </a>
       corridor. Making the US 101 a full
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freeway" title="Freeway">
        freeway
       </a>
       in the 1960s and expansions that followed helped make
       <a href="/wiki/Commuting" title="Commuting">
        commuting
       </a>
       to Los Angeles easier and opened the way for development westward. Development in Ventura County and along the US 101 corridor remains controversial, with open-space advocates battling those who feel business development is necessary to economic growth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although the area still has abundant amount of open space and land, almost all of it was put aside and mandated never to be developed as part of the master plan of each city. Because of this, the area which was once a relatively inexpensive area to buy real estate, saw rising real estate prices well into the 2000s (decade).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Median home prices in the Conejo Valley for instance, now range from $700,000 to $2.2 million.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to
       <a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">
        Forbes
       </a>
       , "it's nearly impossible" to find reasonably priced real estate in California, and the prices will continue to increase.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forbes_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-forbes-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Los Angeles area continues to grow, principally on the periphery where new, cheaper, undeveloped areas are being sought.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halle_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Halle-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As such, in these areas, populations as well as housing prices exploded, although the housing bubble popped late in the decade of the 2000s (decade). Riverside and San Bernardino Counties, which contain large swaths of desert, attracted most of the population increase between 2000 and 2006. Growth continues not only outside the existing
       <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">
        urbanized
       </a>
       area but also adjacent to existing development in the central areas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soja_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Soja-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As in virtually all US core cities, there is now vigorous residential development in the downtown area with both new buildings and renovation of former office buildings. The
       <i>
        Los Angeles Downtown News
       </i>
       keeps a list of ongoing development projects, updated every quarter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Downtown_Los_Angeles-02.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1056" data-file-width="1408" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Downtown_Los_Angeles-02.jpg/220px-Downtown_Los_Angeles-02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Downtown_Los_Angeles-02.jpg/330px-Downtown_Los_Angeles-02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Downtown_Los_Angeles-02.jpg/440px-Downtown_Los_Angeles-02.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Downtown_Los_Angeles-02.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Downtown Los Angeles
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Changes in
       <a href="/wiki/House_price_index" title="House price index">
        house prices
       </a>
       for the area are publicly tracked on a regular basis using the
       <a href="/wiki/Case%E2%80%93Shiller_index" title="Case–Shiller index">
        Case–Shiller index
       </a>
       ; the statistic is published by
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_%26_Poor%27s" title="Standard &amp; Poor's">
        Standard &amp; Poor's
       </a>
       and is also a component of S&amp;P's 10-city
       <a href="/wiki/Composite_(finance)" title="Composite (finance)">
        composite index
       </a>
       of the value of the residential real estate market.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Identity">
        Identity
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Identity">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The term "Greater Los Angeles" can be used to denote the
       <a href="/wiki/Combined_statistical_area" title="Combined statistical area">
        metropolitan area
       </a>
       or the consolidated area. Employment is not only in the
       <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Los_Angeles" title="Downtown Los Angeles">
        downtown
       </a>
       area, but consistently occurs outside the central core. As such, many people commute throughout the city and suburbs in various directions for their work and daily activities, with a large portion heading to the municipalities that are outside the city of Los Angeles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolch_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Wolch-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Unlike most metropolitan areas, regional identity remains a contentious issue in the Greater Los Angeles area, with many residents not acknowledging any association with the region as a whole. For example, while Los Angeles County and
       <a href="/wiki/Orange_County,_California" title="Orange County, California">
        Orange County
       </a>
       together make up the smaller MSA region, the two have a host of sharp demographic, political, and financial distinctions. Orange County residents often attempt to be identified apart from Los Angeles although they make up the same metropolitan area. And while only 1.63% of Los Angeles residents commute to Orange County for work, over 6% of Orange County commuters head to Los Angeles for work.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Western Riverside County and San Bernardino County have become commuter regions characteristic of other suburban counties throughout the nation. Most residents in these counties commute to Los Angeles County and Orange County for employment.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Subregions">
        Subregions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Subregions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LA_at_dawn.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1536" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/LA_at_dawn.jpg/220px-LA_at_dawn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/LA_at_dawn.jpg/330px-LA_at_dawn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/LA_at_dawn.jpg/440px-LA_at_dawn.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LA_at_dawn.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Basin" title="Los Angeles Basin">
          Los Angeles Basin
         </a>
         at dawn
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Los_Angeles">
        Los Angeles
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Los Angeles">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">
        Los Angeles
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       With a population of 3.8 million people as of the 2010 Census, Los Angeles is the second most populous city in the United States after New York City, and is the focal point of the Greater Los Angeles Area.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As an international center for finance, entertainment, media, culture, education, tourism, and science, Los Angeles is considered one of the world's most powerful and influential
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_cities" title="Global cities">
        global cities
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As with some municipalities and areas in the metropolitan region, areas in Los Angeles proper can be defined as being bounded by natural features such as mountains or the ocean; others are marked by city boundaries,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freeway" title="Freeway">
        freeways
       </a>
       , or other constructed landmarks. For example,
       <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Los_Angeles" title="Downtown Los Angeles">
        Downtown Los Angeles
       </a>
       is the area of Los Angeles roughly enclosed by three freeways and one river: the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harbor_Freeway" title="Harbor Freeway">
        Harbor Freeway
       </a>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SR_110_(CA)" title="SR 110 (CA)">
        SR 110
       </a>
       ) to the west, the
       <a href="/wiki/Santa_Ana_Freeway" title="Santa Ana Freeway">
        Santa Ana Freeway
       </a>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Route_101_(California)" title="U.S. Route 101 (California)">
        US 101
       </a>
       ) to the north, the
       <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_River" title="Los Angeles River">
        Los Angeles River
       </a>
       to the east, and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santa_Monica_Freeway" title="Santa Monica Freeway">
        Santa Monica Freeway
       </a>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I-10_(CA)" title="I-10 (CA)">
        I-10
       </a>
       ) to the south.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAT080807_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LAT080807-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Meanwhile, the
       <a href="/wiki/San_Fernando_Valley" title="San Fernando Valley">
        San Fernando Valley
       </a>
       ("The Valley") is defined as the basin consisting of the part of Los Angeles and its suburbs that lie north-northwest of downtown and is ringed by mountains.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other areas of the city of Los Angeles include
       <a href="/wiki/South_Los_Angeles" title="South Los Angeles">
        South Los Angeles
       </a>
       (formerly known officially as South Central L.A.), the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Westside,_Los_Angeles,_California" title="Westside, Los Angeles, California">
        Westside
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harbor_Area" title="Harbor Area">
        Harbor Area
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Los_Angeles_(region)" title="East Los Angeles (region)">
        East Los Angeles
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greater_Hollywood" title="Greater Hollywood">
        Greater Hollywood
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wilshire,_Los_Angeles" title="Wilshire, Los Angeles">
        Wilshire
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Los_Angeles_County">
        Los Angeles County
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Los Angeles County">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_County,_California" title="Los Angeles County, California">
        Los Angeles County, California
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Los Angeles County, of which Los Angeles is the county seat, is the most populous county in the United States and is home to over a quarter of all California residents.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The large size of the city of Los Angeles, as well as its history of annexing smaller towns, has made city boundaries in the central area of Los Angeles County quite complicated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Many cities are completely surrounded by the city of Los Angeles and are often included in the city's areas despite being independent municipalities. For example,
       <a href="/wiki/Santa_Monica,_California" title="Santa Monica, California">
        Santa Monica
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Beverly_Hills,_California" title="Beverly Hills, California">
        Beverly Hills
       </a>
       (which is completely surrounded by Los Angeles) are considered part of the Westside, while
       <a href="/wiki/Hawthorne,_California" title="Hawthorne, California">
        Hawthorne
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Inglewood,_California" title="Inglewood, California">
        Inglewood
       </a>
       are considered associated with South L.A. Adjacent areas that are outside the actual city boundaries of incorporated Los Angeles but border the city itself, include the
       <a href="/wiki/Santa_Clarita_Valley" title="Santa Clarita Valley">
        Santa Clarita Valley
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/San_Gabriel_Valley" title="San Gabriel Valley">
        San Gabriel Valley
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/South_Bay,_Los_Angeles" title="South Bay, Los Angeles">
        South Bay
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Gateway_Cities" title="Gateway Cities">
        Gateway Cities
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Despite the large footprint of the city of Los Angeles, a majority of the land area within Los Angeles County is unincorporated and under the primary jurisdiction of Los Angeles County. Much of this land, however, cannot be easily developed due to planning challenges presented by geographic features such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Santa_Monica_Mountains" title="Santa Monica Mountains">
        Santa Monica Mountains
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/San_Gabriel_Mountains" title="San Gabriel Mountains">
        San Gabriel Mountains range
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Mojave_Desert" title="Mojave Desert">
        Mojave Desert
       </a>
       . Actual land development in these regions occurs on the fringes of incorporated cities, some of which have been fully developed, such as the suburbs of
       <a href="/wiki/Palmdale,_California" title="Palmdale, California">
        Palmdale
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lancaster,_California" title="Lancaster, California">
        Lancaster
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Orange_County">
        Orange County
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Orange County">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Orange_County,_California" title="Orange County, California">
        Orange County, California
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Orange County, together with Los Angeles County, is part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_metropolitan_area" title="Los Angeles metropolitan area">
        Los Angeles metropolitan area
       </a>
       , the inner core of the Greater Los Angeles region. The county is mostly suburban, with no defined urban center. Originally a primarily agricultural area dependent on citrus crops, avocados, and oil extraction, Orange County became a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bedroom_community" title="Bedroom community">
        bedroom community
       </a>
       for Los Angeles upon the completion of numerous freeways linking it to the city, most notably
       <a href="/wiki/Interstate_5_in_California" title="Interstate 5 in California">
        Interstate 5
       </a>
       . The growth of Los Angeles initially fueled population growth in Orange County, but the establishment of the tourism industry allowed it to become an important economic center as well. Today, Orange County is known for its tourist attractions, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Disneyland" title="Disneyland">
        Disneyland
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Knott%27s_Berry_Farm" title="Knott's Berry Farm">
        Knott's Berry Farm
       </a>
       , and its several pristine beaches and coastline. Some larger cities in the county, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Anaheim,_California" title="Anaheim, California">
        Anaheim
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Irvine,_California" title="Irvine, California">
        Irvine
       </a>
       , have evolved to become centers of business and cultural attractions.
      </p>
      <p>
       Orange County is sometimes figuratively divided into "North County" and "South County" by
       <a href="/wiki/California_State_Route_55" title="California State Route 55">
        California State Route 55
       </a>
       (Costa Mesa Freeway). While North County, which includes cities such as Anaheim,
       <a href="/wiki/Fullerton,_California" title="Fullerton, California">
        Fullerton
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Santa_Ana,_California" title="Santa Ana, California">
        Santa Ana
       </a>
       , is the older, more diverse and urbanized area closer to Los Angeles, South County, which includes cities such as
       <a href="/wiki/Laguna_Beach,_California" title="Laguna Beach, California">
        Laguna Beach
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mission_Viejo,_California" title="Mission Viejo, California">
        Mission Viejo
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Newport_Beach,_California" title="Newport Beach, California">
        Newport Beach
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/San_Clemente,_California" title="San Clemente, California">
        San Clemente
       </a>
       , is more affluent, mostly white demographically, and consists of the more recently developed areas to the south and east. An exception to general South County traits is Irvine, which is more diverse than its surrounding cities and is a major center of employment.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ventura_County">
        Ventura County
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ventura County">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Ventura_County,_California" title="Ventura County, California">
        Ventura County, California
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Ventura County is mostly suburban and rural and also has developed primarily through the growth of Los Angeles. The northern part of the county, however, remains largely undeveloped and is mostly within the
       <a href="/wiki/Los_Padres_National_Forest" title="Los Padres National Forest">
        Los Padres National Forest
       </a>
       . Central and southern Ventura County formerly consisted of small towns along the Pacific Coast until the expansion of
       <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_101_in_California" title="U.S. Route 101 in California">
        U.S. Route 101
       </a>
       drew in commuters from the San Fernando Valley. Master-planned cities soon began developing, and the county became increasingly urbanized.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Inland_Empire">
        Inland Empire
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Inland Empire">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inland_Empire_(California)" title="Inland Empire (California)">
        Inland Empire (California)
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Inland Empire, consisting of San Bernardino and Riverside Counties, contains fast-growing suburbs of the region, with a large to majority percentage of the working population commuting to either Los Angeles or Orange Counties for work. Originally an important center for
       <a href="/wiki/Citrus_production" title="Citrus production">
        citrus production
       </a>
       , the region became an important industrial area by the early 20th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-citrus_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-citrus-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Petrix_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Petrix-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorba_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sorba-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Inland Empire also became a key transportation center following the completion of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Route_66_(California)" title="U.S. Route 66 (California)">
        Route 66
       </a>
       , and later
       <a href="/wiki/Interstate_10_in_California" title="Interstate 10 in California">
        Interstate 10
       </a>
       . With the post-World War II economic boom leading to rapid development in Los Angeles and Orange Counties, land developers bulldozed acres of agricultural land to build suburbs in order to accommodate the Los Angeles area's expanding population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-citrus_26-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-citrus-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The development of a regional
       <a href="/wiki/Southern_California_freeways" title="Southern California freeways">
        freeway system
       </a>
       facilitated the expansion of suburbs and human migration linking the Inland Empire and rest of Greater Los Angeles. Despite being primarily suburban, the Inland Empire is also home to important warehousing, shipping, logistics and retail industries, centered on the subregion's major cities of
       <a href="/wiki/Riverside,_California" title="Riverside, California">
        Riverside
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/San_Bernardino,_California" title="San Bernardino, California">
        San Bernardino
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ontario,_California" title="Ontario, California">
        Ontario
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       While the Inland Empire is sometimes defined as the entirety of San Bernardino and Riverside Counties, the eastern undeveloped, desert portions of these counties are not considered to be part of Greater Los Angeles. The state of California defines this area to include the cities of
       <a href="/wiki/Adelanto,_California" title="Adelanto, California">
        Adelanto
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Apple_Valley,_California" title="Apple Valley, California">
        Apple Valley
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Victorville,_California" title="Victorville, California">
        Victorville
       </a>
       to the north, the Riverside–San Diego county line to the south, and the towns of
       <a href="/wiki/Anza,_California" title="Anza, California">
        Anza
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Idyllwild,_California" title="Idyllwild, California">
        Idyllwild
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Lucerne_Valley,_California" title="Lucerne Valley, California">
        Lucerne Valley
       </a>
       , along with the
       <a href="/wiki/San_Bernardino_National_Forest" title="San Bernardino National Forest">
        San Bernardino National Forest
       </a>
       to the east.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, with clear Northern and Southern limits to expansion, the region's urban eastern boundaries have become increasingly nebulous as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suburban_sprawl" title="Suburban sprawl">
        suburban sprawl
       </a>
       continues to spread out to form a unified whole with Los Angeles, with further development encroaching past the
       <a href="/wiki/San_Bernardino_Mountains" title="San Bernardino Mountains">
        San Bernardino
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/San_Jacinto_Mountains" title="San Jacinto Mountains">
        San Jacinto Mountains
       </a>
       and into the outlying desert areas. As a result, the regional definition of Greater Los Angeles can now be extended to include
       <a href="/wiki/Barstow,_California" title="Barstow, California">
        Barstow
       </a>
       and surrounding towns in the northeast, the
       <a href="/wiki/Morongo_Basin" title="Morongo Basin">
        Morongo Basin
       </a>
       in the east-central including
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yucca_Valley,_CA" title="Yucca Valley, CA">
        Yucca Valley
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Twentynine_Palms,_California" title="Twentynine Palms, California">
        Twentynine Palms
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Coachella_Valley" title="Coachella Valley">
        Coachella Valley
       </a>
       cities in the southeast. This interconnectivity, provided by one of the most extensive freeway systems in the world, as well as economic, social and media ties has blended boundaries between these regions and the urbanized Los Angeles and Inland Empire areas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosenblatt_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Rosenblatt-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Areas_included_by_Census_Bureau">
        Areas included by Census Bureau
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Areas included by Census Bureau">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       While the above areas are included in the regional definition of Greater Los Angeles, the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">
        U.S. Census Bureau
       </a>
       defines Greater Los Angeles, or officially, the Los Angeles-Long Beach Combined Statistical Area, to include both the above-mentioned areas along with the entirety of San Bernardino and Riverside counties.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OMB_10-02_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-OMB_10-02-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These areas are sparsely developed and are part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Mojave_Desert" title="Mojave Desert">
        Mojave
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Desert" title="Colorado Desert">
        Colorado Deserts
       </a>
       . To the north,
       <a href="/wiki/Interstate_15_in_California" title="Interstate 15 in California">
        Interstate 15
       </a>
       crosses desolate desert landscape after passing Barstow, linking Greater Los Angeles with
       <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas" title="Las Vegas">
        Las Vegas
       </a>
       , with
       <a href="/wiki/Baker,_California" title="Baker, California">
        Baker
       </a>
       being the only significant outpost along the route. To the east, lie the
       <a href="/wiki/Mojave_National_Preserve" title="Mojave National Preserve">
        Mojave National Preserve
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Tree_National_Park" title="Joshua Tree National Park">
        Joshua Tree National Park
       </a>
       along with the towns of
       <a href="/wiki/Needles,_California" title="Needles, California">
        Needles
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Blythe,_California" title="Blythe, California">
        Blythe
       </a>
       on the California-Arizona border.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Census_statistics">
        Census statistics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Census statistics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GreaterLAmap.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/GreaterLAmap.png/250px-GreaterLAmap.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/GreaterLAmap.png/375px-GreaterLAmap.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/GreaterLAmap.png/500px-GreaterLAmap.png 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GreaterLAmap.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Map showing
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combined_Statistical_Area" title="Combined Statistical Area">
          CSA
         </a>
         ,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Statistical_Area" title="Metropolitan Statistical Area">
          MSAs
         </a>
         , and
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urban_Area" title="Urban Area">
          UAs
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Combined_Statistical_Area">
        Combined Statistical Area
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Combined Statistical Area">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Los Angeles–Long Beach Combined Statistical Area consists of the entirety of the counties of Los Angeles, Orange, Ventura, San Bernardino, and Riverside, including the sparsely developed eastern portions of the latter two that are not included in the regional definition of Greater Los Angeles. As of the 2010 United States Census, it is home to nearly half of all California residents.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popEst_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-popEst-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Metropolitan_Statistical_Areas">
        Metropolitan Statistical Areas
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Metropolitan Statistical Areas">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Metropolitan_Statistical_Areas" title="List of Metropolitan Statistical Areas">
        List of Metropolitan Statistical Areas
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_metropolitan_area" title="Los Angeles metropolitan area">
        Los Angeles metropolitan area
       </a>
       comprises Los Angeles County (2010 population: 9,862,049) and
       <a href="/wiki/Orange_County,_California" title="Orange County, California">
        Orange County
       </a>
       (2010 population: 3,010,759). It is officially designated by the US Census Bureau as the Los Angeles–Long Beach–Anaheim, CA Metropolitan Statistical Area. The total population for the Los Angeles metropolitan area at the 2010 Census was 12,872,808.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popcsa_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-popcsa-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In addition to the Los Angeles–Long Beach–Anaheim, CA Metropolitan Statistical Area, the following
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Statistical_Area" title="Metropolitan Statistical Area">
        Metropolitan Statistical Areas
       </a>
       are also included in the Los Angeles-Long Beach, CA Combined Statistical Area:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-popcsa_33-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-popcsa-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Ventura_County,_California#Metropolitan_Statistical_Area" title="Ventura County, California">
        Oxnard–Thousand Oaks–Ventura, CA Metropolitan Statistical Area
       </a>
       (2010 population: 797,740), within
       <a href="/wiki/Ventura_County,_California" title="Ventura County, California">
        Ventura County
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Inland_Empire" title="Inland Empire">
        Riverside–San Bernardino–Ontario, CA Metropolitan Statistical Area
       </a>
       (2010 population: 4,115,871), made up of
       <a href="/wiki/Riverside_County,_California" title="Riverside County, California">
        Riverside County
       </a>
       (2010 population: 2,100,516) and
       <a href="/wiki/San_Bernardino_County,_California" title="San Bernardino County, California">
        San Bernardino County
       </a>
       (2010 population: 2,015,355).
      </p>
      <h4>

       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Oxnard–Thousand_Oaks–Ventura_MSA">
        Oxnard–Thousand Oaks–Ventura MSA
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Oxnard–Thousand Oaks–Ventura MSA">

        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>

      <p>
       According to the 2010 census, there were 17,877,006 people residing in the Greater Los Angeles Area. The racial makeup of the area was 54.9%
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_(U.S._Census)" title="White (U.S. Census)">
        White
       </a>
       (39.0% White Non-Hispanic), 12.3%
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_(U.S._Census)" title="Asian (U.S. Census)">
        Asian
       </a>
       , 0.3%
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific_Islander_(U.S._Census)" title="Pacific Islander (U.S. Census)">
        Pacific Islander
       </a>
       , 7.0%
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American_(U.S._Census)" title="African American (U.S. Census)">
        African American
       </a>
       , 0.8%
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_American_(U.S._Census)" title="Native American (U.S. Census)">
        Native American
       </a>
       , 20.2% from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(U.S._Census)" title="Race (U.S. Census)">
        other races
       </a>
       , and 4.5% from two or more races. 44.9% of the population (8.0 million) were
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hispanic_(U.S._Census)" title="Hispanic (U.S. Census)">
        Hispanic
       </a>
       of any race, including 35.7% of the population (6.4 million) which was of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_American" title="Mexican American">
        Mexican
       </a>
       origin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       31.0% of the population (5.5 million) was foreign born; 18.3% (3.3 million) came from Latin America and 9.8% (1.7 million) from Asia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The explosive growth of the region in the 20th century can be attributed to its favorable
       <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_climate" title="Mediterranean climate">
        Mediterranean climate
       </a>
       , the availability of land and many booming industries such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_industry" title="Oil industry">
        oil
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile_industry" title="Automobile industry">
        automobile
       </a>
       and rubber,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Movie_industry" title="Movie industry">
        motion pictures
       </a>
       and aerospace which in turn attracted millions of people from all over the United States and world.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2010)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Citrus production was important to the region's development in the earlier part of the 20th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garcia_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Garcia-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       While the
       <a href="/wiki/New_York_metropolitan_area" title="New York metropolitan area">
        New York metropolitan area
       </a>
       is presently the most populous metropolitan area in the United States, it has been predicted in the past that Greater Los Angeles will eventually surpass Greater New York in population.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2010)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Whether this will happen is yet to be seen, but past predictions on this event have been off the mark. A 1966 article in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">
         Time
        </a>
       </i>
       predicted Greater Los Angeles would surpass New York by 1975, and that by 1990, would reach close to the 19 million mark.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       But the article's flawed definition of Greater Los Angeles included
       <a href="/wiki/San_Diego" title="San Diego">
        San Diego
       </a>
       , which is actually its own
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Diego-Carlsbad-San_Marcos,_CA_MSA" title="San Diego-Carlsbad-San Marcos, CA MSA">
        metropolitan area
       </a>
       . A 1989 article in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">
         The New York Times
        </a>
       </i>
       predicted Greater Los Angeles would surpass Greater New York by 2010,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but the article predicted the population would be 18.3 million in that year, a number Greater New York has already surpassed as of 2007 by half a million people. As of 2009, the New York metropolitan area had a population of 22.2 million compared to the Greater Los Angeles Area's 18.7 million, about a 3.56 million persons difference.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Percentage growth, however, has been higher in Greater Los Angeles over the past few decades than in Greater New York.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Regions_of_the_Greater_Los_Angeles_Area">
        Regions of the Greater Los Angeles Area
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Regions of the Greater Los Angeles Area">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:California_Poppies3.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/California_Poppies3.jpg/220px-California_Poppies3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/California_Poppies3.jpg/330px-California_Poppies3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/California_Poppies3.jpg/440px-California_Poppies3.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:California_Poppies3.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/California_poppy" title="California poppy">
          California poppies
         </a>
         in
         <a href="/wiki/Antelope_Valley_California_Poppy_Reserve" title="Antelope Valley California Poppy Reserve">
          Antelope Valley California Poppy Reserve
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       While there is not official designation for the regions that comprise Greater Los Angeles, one authority, the
       <i>
        Los Angeles Times
       </i>
       , divides the area into the following regions:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Angeles_National_Forest" title="Angeles National Forest">
         Angeles Forest
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Antelope_Valley" title="Antelope Valley">
         Antelope Valley
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        Central L.A. (
        <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Los_Angeles" title="Downtown Los Angeles">
         Downtown Los Angeles
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Hollywood" title="Hollywood">
         Hollywood
        </a>
        , etc.)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Los_Angeles_(region)" title="East Los Angeles (region)">
         Eastside
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        Harbor (see
        <a href="/wiki/Gateway_Cities" title="Gateway Cities">
         Gateway Cities
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Northeast L.A. (
        <a href="/wiki/Highland_Park,_Los_Angeles" title="Highland Park, Los Angeles">
         Highland Park
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Eagle_Rock,_Los_Angeles" title="Eagle Rock, Los Angeles">
         Eagle Rock
        </a>
        , etc.)
       </li>
       <li>
        Northwest County (including the
        <a href="/wiki/Santa_Clarita_Valley" title="Santa Clarita Valley">
         Santa Clarita Valley
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Pomona_Valley" title="Pomona Valley">
         Pomona Valley
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/San_Fernando_Valley" title="San Fernando Valley">
         San Fernando Valley
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/San_Gabriel_Valley" title="San Gabriel Valley">
         San Gabriel Valley
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Santa_Monica_Mountains" title="Santa Monica Mountains">
         Santa Monica Mountains
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Malibu,_California" title="Malibu, California">
         Malibu
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Topanga,_California" title="Topanga, California">
         Topanga
        </a>
        , etc.)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/South_Bay,_Los_Angeles" title="South Bay, Los Angeles">
         South Bay
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/South_Los_Angeles" title="South Los Angeles">
         South Los Angeles
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        Southeast Los Angeles County (including
        <a href="/wiki/Norwalk,_California" title="Norwalk, California">
         Norwalk
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Whittier,_California" title="Whittier, California">
         Whittier
        </a>
        , see
        <a href="/wiki/Gateway_Cities" title="Gateway Cities">
         Gateway Cities
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        The Verdugos (including
        <a href="/wiki/Glendale,_California" title="Glendale, California">
         Glendale
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Pasadena,_California" title="Pasadena, California">
         Pasadena
        </a>
        and the
        <a href="/wiki/Crescenta_Valley" title="Crescenta Valley">
         Crescenta Valley
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Westside_(Los_Angeles_County)" title="Westside (Los Angeles County)">
         Westside
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Regions in adjacent counties include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Orange_County,_California" title="Orange County, California">
         Orange County
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inland_Empire,_California" title="Inland Empire, California">
         Inland Empire
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Conejo_Valley" title="Conejo Valley">
         Conejo Valley
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Oxnard_Plain" title="Oxnard Plain">
         Oxnard Plain
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/High_Desert_(California)" title="High Desert (California)">
         High Desert
        </a>
        (includes
        <a href="/wiki/Antelope_Valley" title="Antelope Valley">
         Antelope Valley
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victor_Valley,_California" title="Victor Valley, California">
         Victor Valley
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Morongo_Basin" title="Morongo Basin">
         Morongo Basin
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Low_Desert" title="Low Desert">
         Low Desert
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Coachella_Valley" title="Coachella Valley">
         Coachella Valley
        </a>
        )
       </li>
      </ul>

      <p>
       Greater Los Angeles is a politically divided metropolitan area. During the 1970s and 1980s the region leaned toward the
       <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">
        Republican Party
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_County,_California" title="Los Angeles County, California">
        Los Angeles County
       </a>
       , the most populous of the region, is a
       <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">
        Democratic
       </a>
       stronghold, although it voted twice for both Richard Nixon (1968 and 1972) and Ronald Reagan (1980 and 1984).
       <a href="/wiki/Riverside_County,_California" title="Riverside County, California">
        Riverside County
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/San_Bernardino_County,_California" title="San Bernardino County, California">
        San Bernardino County
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Orange_County,_California" title="Orange County, California">
        Orange County
       </a>
       lean toward the Republican Party.
       <a href="/wiki/Ventura_County,_California" title="Ventura County, California">
        Ventura County
       </a>
       is politically divided.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">
        Economy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Economy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The Greater Los Angeles Area has the third largest metropolitan economy in the world, behind
       <a href="/wiki/Greater_Tokyo_Area" title="Greater Tokyo Area">
        Greater Tokyo Area
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Metropolitan_Area" title="New York Metropolitan Area">
        New York Metropolitan Area
       </a>
       . A 2010 Greyhill Advisors study indicated that the Los Angeles metropolitan area had a gross metropolitan product of $736 billion.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Greater Los Angeles (including the Inland Empire and
       <a href="/wiki/Ventura_County,_California" title="Ventura County, California">
        Ventura County
       </a>
       ) had a $770.6 billion economy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Greater Los Angeles Area is the home of the US national headquarters of almost all Asian major car manufacturers except
       <a href="/wiki/Nissan" title="Nissan">
        Nissan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">
        Toyota
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Subaru" title="Subaru">
        Subaru
       </a>
       (Nissan moved to
       <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">
        Tennessee
       </a>
       ; Toyota moved to
       <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">
        Texas
       </a>
       ; Subaru first located in
       <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">
        Philadelphia
       </a>
       but moved to
       <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">
        New Jersey
       </a>
       ); Honda, Mazda, Mitsubishi, Suzuki, Hyundai and Kia have set up their national headquarters here.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Entertainment">
        Entertainment
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Entertainment">

        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Promoted as the "
       <a href="/wiki/The_Entertainment_Capital_of_the_World" title="The Entertainment Capital of the World">
        Entertainment Capital of the World
       </a>
       ", Greater Los Angeles is one of the world's largest tourist destinations. Major attractions include:
      </p>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="BEA_economic_area">
        BEA economic area
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: BEA economic area">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Economic_Analysis" title="Bureau of Economic Analysis">
        Bureau of Economic Analysis
       </a>
       (BEA) defines an even larger region known as an "economic area" (EA), which delineates the relevant regional market surrounding a metropolitan area. "BEA's economic areas define the relevant regional markets surrounding metropolitan or micropolitan statistical areas. They consist of one or more economic nodes – metropolitan or micropolitan statistical areas that serve as regional centers of economic activity – and the surrounding counties that are economically related to the nodes." The Los Angeles economic area consists of the Los Angeles–Long Beach Combined Statistical Area and includes the California counties of Imperial, Kern, Orange, Riverside, San Bernardino, San Luis Obispo, Santa Barbara, and Ventura, as well as La Paz County and Yuma County in Arizona.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Major_business_districts">
        Major business districts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Major business districts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Greater Los Angeles supports large
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Business_districts" title="Business districts">
        business districts
       </a>
       throughout its urban area. The
       <a href="/wiki/Central_business_district" title="Central business district">
        central business district
       </a>
       is located at
       <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Los_Angeles" title="Downtown Los Angeles">
        Downtown Los Angeles
       </a>
       . Within the Los Angeles city limits are multiple districts, and other than
       <a href="/wiki/Bunker_Hill,_Los_Angeles" title="Bunker Hill, Los Angeles">
        Bunker Hill
       </a>
       , these are
       <a href="/wiki/Century_City,_Los_Angeles" title="Century City, Los Angeles">
        Century City
       </a>
       and businesses lining
       <a href="/wiki/Wilshire_Boulevard" title="Wilshire Boulevard">
        Wilshire Boulevard
       </a>
       . Other major districts nearby Los Angeles include
       <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Long_Beach" title="Downtown Long Beach">
        Downtown Long Beach
       </a>
       , downtown Glendale, and downtown
       <a href="/wiki/Burbank,_California" title="Burbank, California">
        Burbank
       </a>
       . In the southern reaches of Greater Los Angeles, major business districts include
       <a href="/wiki/Newport_Center" title="Newport Center">
        Newport Center
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/South_Coast_Metro" title="South Coast Metro">
        South Coast Metro
       </a>
       , and the developing business district in
       <a href="/wiki/Irvine,_California" title="Irvine, California">
        Irvine
       </a>
       . To the east major business districts include the respective centers of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downtown_Riverside" title="Downtown Riverside">
        Downtown Riverside
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Downtown_San_Bernardino" title="Downtown San Bernardino">
        Downtown San Bernardino
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">
        Transportation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Greater_Los_Angeles&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Transportation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Greater Los Angeles is known for its expansive transportation network. Most notable is its extensive highway system. The area is a junction for numerous interstates coming from the north, east, and south and contains the three principal north-south highways in California:
       <a href="/wiki/Interstate_5" title="Interstate 5">
        Interstate 5
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_101" title="U.S. Route 101">
        U.S. Route 101
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/California_State_Route_1" title="California State Route 1">
        California State Route 1
       </a>
       . The area is also home to several ports, including the twin ports of
       <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Long_Beach" title="Port of Long Beach">
        Long Beach
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Los_Angeles" title="Port of Los Angeles">
        Los Angeles
       </a>
       , which are the two busiest in the United States, as well as
       <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Hueneme" title="Port of Hueneme">
        Port of Hueneme
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAT20110807_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LAT20110807-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Additionally, the region is also served by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metro_Rail_(Los_Angeles_County)" title="Metro Rail (Los Angeles County)">
        Metrorail
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metrolink_(Southern_California)" title="Metrolink (Southern California)">
        Metrolink
       </a>
       commuter rail systems that link neighborhoods of Los Angeles with immediate surrounding suburbs and most of the region (excluding the outer region of the Inland Empire) with
       <a href="/wiki/Oceanside,_California" title="Oceanside, California">
        Oceanside
       </a>
       in San Diego County, respectively.
       <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_International_Airport" title="Los Angeles International Airport">
        Los Angeles International Airport
       </a>
       (LAX) is the principal international airport of the region and is among one of the busiest in the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other airports include
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LA/Ontario_International_Airport" title="LA/Ontario International Airport">
        LA/Ontario International Airport
       </a>
       (ONT),
       <a href="/wiki/John_Wayne_Airport" title="John Wayne Airport">
        John Wayne Airport
       </a>
       (SNA),
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bob_Hope_Airport" title="Bob Hope Airport">
        Bob Hope Airport
       </a>
       (BUR),
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_Beach_Municipal_Airport" title="Long Beach Municipal Airport">
        Long Beach Municipal Airport
       </a>
       (LGB), and
       <a href="/wiki/Palm_Springs_International_Airport" title="Palm Springs International Airport">
        Palm Springs International Airport
       </a>
       (PSP).
      </p>
